        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.2c10915"><meta property="og:title" content="Promoting Fast Na Ion Transport at Low Temperatures for Sodium Metal Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/asset/images/acsami.2c10915.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Cost concerns have promoted the rise of Na-based batteries as an alternative to Li-based batteries, and the energy density pursuits have brought attention to Na metal anodes. Numerous studies have been conducted on the failure mechanisms and improvement methods of Na metal batteries (NMBs) at room temperature; however, the low-temperature applications are still faced with more complex challenges. On the basis of the concentration effect of the electrolytes, we propose a dilute electrolyte for the low-temperature operation of NMBs. With the low salt concentration and tetrahydrofuran cosolvent, the affinity between Na ions and the solvent molecules is weakened to achieve fast reaction kinetics at low temperatures. The designed electrolyte enables an effectively decreased cell impedance, which improves the cycling stability of Na symmetric cells. As a result, the full cell paired with the Na3V2(PO4)3 cathode maintains a capacity of 80 mAh/g over 250 cycles at −20 °C. This work provides an electrolyte design strategy toward low-temperature NMBs."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/asset/images/acsami.2c10915.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90da896db8a1b325-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da896db8a1b325-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'd9fddfb7-dea8-4be9-a935-fa085b71ceda'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da896db8a1b325-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Promoting Fast Na Ion Transport at Low Temperatures for Sodium Metal Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c10915", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Promoting Fast Na Ion Transport at Low Temperatures for Sodium Metal Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c10915", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;

                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });

                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Promoting Fast Na Ion Transport at Low Temperatures for Sodium Metal Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c10915", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Promoting Fast Na Ion Transport at Low Temperatures for Sodium Metal Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c10915", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Promoting Fast Na Ion Transport at Low Temperatures for Sodium Metal Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c10915", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Promoting Fast Na Ion Transport at Low Temperatures for Sodium Metal Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c10915", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Promoting Fast Na Ion Transport at Low Temperatures for Sodium Metal Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c10915", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Promoting Fast Na Ion Transport at Low Temperatures for Sodium Metal Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c10915", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/asset/images/medium/am2c10915_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/asset/images/large/am2c10915_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c10915&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Cost concerns have promoted the rise of Na-based batteries as an alternative to Li-based batteries, and the energy density pursuits have brought attention to Na metal anodes. Numerous studies have been conducted on the failure mechanisms and improvement methods of Na metal batteries (NMBs) at room temperature; however, the low-temperature applications are still faced with more complex challenges. On the basis of the concentration effect of the electrolytes, we propose a dilute electrolyte for the low-temperature operation of NMBs. With the low salt concentration and tetrahydrofuran cosolvent, the affinity between Na ions and the solvent molecules is weakened to achieve fast reaction kinetics at low temperatures. The designed electrolyte enables an effectively decreased cell impedance, which improves the cycling stability of Na symmetric cells. As a result, the full cell paired with the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode maintains a capacity of 80 mAh/g over 250 cycles at −20 °C. This work provides an electrolyte design strategy toward low-temperature NMBs.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D40985%26pageCount%3D7%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DZhongqiang%2BWang%252C%2BXueying%2BZheng%252C%2BXuyang%2BLiu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D14%26issueNum%3D36%26contentID%3Dacsami.2c10915%26title%3DPromoting%2BFast%2BNa%2BIon%2BTransport%2Bat%2BLow%2BTemperatures%2Bfor%2BSodium%2BMetal%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D7%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D40991%26publicationDate%3DSeptember%2B2022" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2022 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292120&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrolytes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrolytes"                     </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" temperature</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Weak+Solvation&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_weak%20solvation">weak solvation</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The ever-growing demands for electric vehicles, energy storage, and other battery-intense applications have skyrocketed the prices of lithium raw materials, such as lithium carbonate (Li<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>), in parallel with the thriving lithium ion batteries (LIBs) market. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> Motivated by the natural abundance, wide geographical availability, and low cost of sodium-containing resources, sodium ion batteries (SIBs) are considered a promising alternative to LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3 ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 3 and 4">(3,4)</a> The carbonaceous anode is a key ingredient in LIBs or SIBs; however, it delivers a relatively low specific capacity and significantly limits batteries’ energy density. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a> On the other hand, metallic Li has triggered renewed interest in recent years since its ∼10 times higher specific capacity than the standard graphite anode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 6 and 7">(6,7)</a> Similarly, among the numerous anodes, the Na metal anodes (NMAs) stand out as one of the most potential candidates for high-energy Na-based batteries because of their high theoretical specific capacity (1166 mAh/g) and low redox potential (−2.71 V vs S.H.E.). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 8 and 9">(8,9)</a> Unfortunately, the highly reactive NMAs suffer from unstable solid–electrolyte interphase (SEI) and severe dendrite growth with conventional carbonate-based organic liquid electrolytes, which results in a low Coulombic efficiency (CE) and short serving life. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref11 ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 10 to 12">(10−12)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Recent cryo-electron microscopy studies have shown the atomic structures of SEI on Na metal surfaces and revealed that SEI plays a critical role in stabilizing metallic anodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14 ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 13 to 15">(13−15)</a> Over the past few years, tremendous efforts have been devoted to constructing a stable SEI with effective passivation. Among them, electrolyte engineering is the key. For example, utilization of the ether solvents, including 1,2-dimethoxyethane, diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (DEGDME), and tetrahydrofuran (THF), has been reported to enable the highly durable NMAs due to their high LUMO and Na compatibility. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 16 and 17">(16,17)</a> In addition, introducing additives such as KTFSI <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> and FEC <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> is also effective. These works have effectively regulated the Na deposition/stripping behavior and enhanced the electrochemical performance of NMBs at room temperature. Nonetheless, low-temperature (LT) applications of NMAs remain more complex challenges but largely unexplored. The dramatically increasing viscosity of liquid electrolytes below 0 °C results in poor wettability and sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21 ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 20 to 22">(20−22)</a> Additionally, the deteriorative mass transfer capability at the electrode/electrolyte interphase leads to severe concentration polarization, raising inhomogeneous deposition and Na dendrite growth at LT. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 23 and 24">(23,24)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">In the past several years, increasing the salt concentration of electrolytes has been developed to expand the electrochemical window of electrolytes and generate functional SEI for metallic anodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 25 and 26">(25,26)</a> Recently, instead of increasing the concentration, Hu’s group proposed an ultralow concentration electrolyte for SIBs to broaden the operating temperature range. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> Herein, we use this methodology for a novel approach toward LT NMBs by a dilute ether-based electrolyte (0.3 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DEGDME/THF) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>). With the lower concentration, the electrolyte maintained a low viscosity and sufficient ionic conductivity at LT. Compared to the harsh kinetics of conventional 1.0 M carbonate electrolyte, the dilute electrolyte enabled a reduced impedance (10 Ω) and polarization voltage (∼0.01 V) at −20 °C, accompanied by improved Na deposition morphology. As a result, the Na/Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cell with the dilute electrolyte could deliver a specific capacity of ∼80 mAh/g and maintain a much lower overpotential at −20 °C. Together, these results show the feasibility of the low concentration electrolyte strategy for LT NMBs.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/asset/images/medium/am2c10915_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/asset/images/large/am2c10915_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic of the dilute ether-based electrolyte for Na metal anodes at LT. The viscosity and ionic conductivity degradation as the temperature decreases is alleviated by the low salt concentration. As a step forward, the binding between the Na<sup>+</sup> ions and solvents is weakened, resulting in fast reaction kinetics at LT.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/asset/images/large/am2c10915_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c10915&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Ether-based solvents were adopted in this study because of their high compatibility with NMAs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> However, the typical ether-based electrolyte (1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DEGDME) solidifies at −20 °C (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/suppl_file/am2c10915_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>) and is thus impracticable for LT applications. To avoid undesired freezing and keep the electrolyte low viscosity and sufficient ionic conductivity at LT, the salt concentration was reduced to 0.3 M (denoted as 0.3-D). Furthermore, the cyclic ether THF with a low melting point (108.4 °C) and weak solvation ability was introduced as a cosolvent, obtaining the recipe of 0.3 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DEGDME/THF electrolyte (denoted as 0.3-D/T). On the basis of the optimized low-temperature performance of Na/Na symmetric cells by using different THF concentrations (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/suppl_file/am2c10915_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>), the DEGDME/THF ratio was thus fixed at 7:3 where the overpotential was restricted to only 0.016 mV at 2.0 mA/cm<sup>2</sup>. 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in ethylene carbonate (EC)/diethylene carbonate (DEC) (1.0-E/D<sub>C</sub>), as a conventional carbonate electrolyte, was selected as a control. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a, the temperature dropping resulted in increased viscosity and reduced ionic conductivity for these electrolytes. The superiority of the as-designed dilute electrolytes was exerted with the temperature declining below 0 °C. The 0.3-D/T electrolyte showed a low viscosity (18.1 mPa·s) at −20 °C, whereas the conventional 1.0-E/D<sub>C</sub> was recorded with a dramatically increased viscosity (74.8 mPa·s). Furthermore, when the temperature was lowered to −30 °C, the 1.0-E/D<sub>C</sub> electrolyte became frozen while the 0.3-D and 0.3-D/T counterparts still maintained their liquid phase even as the temperature decreased to −40 °C. Benefiting from the lower viscosity, the 0.3-D/T electrolyte exhibited a smaller contact angle (27°) with the polypropylene (PP) separator, in comparison with the 1.0-E/Dc (46°), unveiling the enhanced wettability (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b,c). Meanwhile, the dilute electrolytes were detected with moderate ionic conductivity at room temperature because of the lower carrier number. However, when the temperature dropped to −20 °C, the ionic conductivity of 0.3-D/T reached up to 2.1 mS/cm, which outperformed 1.0-E/Dc since the lower viscosity.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/asset/images/medium/am2c10915_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/asset/images/large/am2c10915_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) Viscosity and ionic conductivity of the 0.3-D/T, 0.3-D, and 1.0-E/D<sub>C</sub> electrolytes at temperatures ranging from −40 to 25 °C. The contact angle of (b) the 0.3-D/T or (c) the 1.0-E/D<sub>C</sub> electrolyte on PP separators. MD simulation snapshots of the (d) 0.3-D/T, (e) 0.3-D, and (f) 1.0-E/D<sub>C</sub> electrolytes. (g) Binding energy between Na<sup>+</sup> and the solvent molecules in different electrolytes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/asset/images/large/am2c10915_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c10915&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">For a deep understanding of the solvation chemistry in corresponding electrolytes, molecular dynamic (MD) simulations were conducted. As seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d,f, fewer Na<sup>+</sup>/solvent clusters can be observed from the snapshot of 0.3-D/T due to the lower concentration, in comparison with the conventional 1.0-E/Dc. The radial distribution functions (RDFs) of the electrolytes were collected to probe the solvation sheath based on the MD simulation. At the distance between 0 and 3 Å, corresponding to the first solvation sheath, a sharper peak of Na–O<sub>EC</sub> was recorded than that of Na–O<sub>DEC</sub> and Na–P<sub>PF6-</sub> due to the tighter binding between Na<sup>+</sup> ions and EC molecules (−1.73 eV) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/suppl_file/am2c10915_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). With regard to the 0.3-D/T electrolyte, no peaks of THF solvents can be seen within 3 Å (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/suppl_file/am2c10915_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>), indicating THF hardly entered the solvation sheath of Na<sup>+</sup> and were distributed as free solvents. Consequently, the DEGDME solvent dominated the Na<sup>+</sup> ion solvation sheath in the 0.3-D/T electrolyte, which was similar to 0.3-D (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/suppl_file/am2c10915_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). However, the binding energy of Na<sup>+</sup>-DEGDME in 0.3-D/T was lower than that of 0.3-D electrolyte, indicating that the introduction of THF effectively weakened the binding between Na<sup>+</sup> and DEGDME, which further speeded up the desolvation process of 0.3-D/T.</div><div class="NLM_p">Next, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) analysis was conducted to evaluate the Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport kinetics in the Na symmetric cells with different electrolytes. In the Nyquist plots given in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a, the impedance of the cell coupling with the 0.3-D/T electrolyte (&lt;5 Ω) was lower than that of the conventional 1.0-E/D<sub>C</sub> counterpart (∼200 Ω) at room temperature, deriving from the fewer side reactions and SEI accumulation in the ether-based electrolyte. Strikingly, when lowering the temperature to −20 °C, the cell impedance reached up to &gt;4000 Ω when the 1.0-E/D<sub>C</sub> electrolyte was used (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b). Nevertheless, the dilute 0.3-D electrolyte enabled a low impedance of 22 Ω. Noteworthily, the introduction of the THF cosolvent further lowered the cell impedance to 10 Ω, implying the facilitated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion when using the 0.3-D/T electrolyte. Furthermore, the charge transfer impedance (<i>R</i><sub>CT</sub>) and the ion transport impedance in SEI (<i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>) corresponding to the mid- and high-frequency region, respectively, were obtained by fitting the temperature-dependent EIS spectra (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/suppl_file/am2c10915_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>) with the equivalent circuit given in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/suppl_file/am2c10915_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> The Arrhenius equation <span class="NLM_inline-formula"><i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer inline-formula-styles" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/asset/images/medium/am2c10915_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i4"></span> was then utilized to calculate the activation energies, where <i>A</i>, <i>E</i><sub>a</sub>, and <i>R</i> stand for the precession constant, activation energy, and ideal gas constant, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> The activation energies of desolvation and interfacial Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in SEI were termed <i>E</i><sub>a1</sub> and <i>E</i><sub>a2</sub>, respectively. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>c and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d, <i>E</i><sub>a1</sub> and <i>E</i><sub>a2</sub> for the 1.0-E/D<sub>C</sub> electrolyte were calculated to be 76.16 and 52.55 kJ/mol, stemming from the high affinity of Na<sup>+</sup>/carbonate-solvent and the poor SEI. Replacing the 1.0-E/D<sub>C</sub> electrolyte with the 0.3-D electrolyte reduced <i>E</i><sub>a1</sub> and <i>E</i><sub>a2</sub> to 19.15 and 17.76 kJ/mol due to the lower binding energy of Na<sup>+</sup>/DEGDME and improved SEI chemistry in the ether-based electrolyte. Furthermore, the 0.3-D/T electrolyte possessed even smaller <i>E</i><sub>a1</sub> and <i>E</i><sub>a2</sub> (6.82 and 13.81 kJ/mol), implying the further accelerated Na<sup>+</sup> ion desolvation process and transport kinetics through SEI. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> Meanwhile, the bulk resistance reflected by the ultrahigh-frequency region intercept only accounts for a marginal part of the total impedance at LT, demonstrating that the relatively low carrier number of the dilute electrolytes hardly posed a negative effect at LT. Facilitated by the improved reaction kinetics, the barriers for Na deposition have been obviously reduced, as reflected in the nucleation overpotentials given in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/suppl_file/am2c10915_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>. At room temperature, the nucleation overpotential got reduced from 133.3 to 23.0 mV when replacing the 1.0-E/D<sub>C</sub> electrolyte with 0.3-D and then further decreased to 18.0 mV by using the 0.3-D/T electrolyte. As the temperature decreased to −20 °C, the 1.0-E/D<sub>C</sub>, 0.3-D, and 0.3-D/T samples exhibited a nucleation overpotential of 202.1, 34.4, and 20.7 mV, respectively, indicating that the 0.3-D/T electrolyte afforded the lowest nucleation energy barrier. Moreover, the mass-transfer overpotential, depicted by the flat part of the voltage plateau, was also much lower in the 0.3-D/T system than that obtained by using 1.0-E/D<sub>C</sub>, which reveals a promoted nuclei growth process.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/asset/images/medium/am2c10915_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/asset/images/large/am2c10915_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Nyquist plots of the Na/Na symmetric cells after three formation cycles with 1 mA/cm<sup>2</sup> and 1 mAh/cm<sup>2</sup> at (a) 25 °C and (b) −20 °C. Fitting results according to the Arrhenius equation of (c) <i>R</i><sub>CT</sub> and (d) <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> extracted from the impedance spectra of each battery system at different temperatures, in which the corresponding activation energies are marked.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/asset/images/large/am2c10915_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c10915&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further investigate Na deposition behavior with different electrolytes, the top-view scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images of the Na metal electrodes cycled at −20 °C were collected. The morphology of Na deposition in the 1.0-E/D<sub>C</sub> electrolyte was overwhelmed by a mass of whiskers and pores (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a), implying an uneven Na<sup>+</sup> flux caused by high concentration polarization. When operating in the 0.3-D electrolyte, the deposited Na appeared to be less dendritic but covered with numerous grooves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b). In comparison, a homogeneous and dendrite-free Na deposition layer was formed in 0.3-D/T (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c). To assess the durability of Na metal electrodes in corresponding electrolytes, the Na/Na symmetric cells were fabricated. When coupling with the 1.0-E/D<sub>C</sub> electrolyte, the symmetric cell exhibited a large and unstable polarization at room temperature, while the dilute electrolytes enabled the stable cycle with smaller overpotentials (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/suppl_file/am2c10915_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>). As the temperature decreased to −20 °C, in accordance with the porous Na deposition, the symmetric cell with the 1.0-E/D<sub>C</sub> electrolyte experienced a rapid failure (&lt;100 h) along with the spiking voltage hysteresis (&gt;1 V). By utilizing the 0.3-D electrolyte, the life span could be prolonged to about 1000 h, whereas the cell underwent a gradually enlarged overpotential after 500 h cycling (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d). In sharp contrast, the cell with the 0.3-D/T electrolyte delivered an ultrastable voltage profile over 1000 h, accompanied by a lower overpotential (∼10 mV), which further verified the accelerated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and improved plating/stripping behaviors.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/asset/images/medium/am2c10915_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/asset/images/large/am2c10915_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Top-view SEM images of Na deposition at −20 °C with (a) 1.0-E/D<sub>C</sub>, (b) 0.3-D, and (c) 0.3-D/T electrolytes. (d) Voltage profile of Na/Na symmetric cells with corresponding electrolytes cycling at 1 mA/cm<sup>2</sup> with a capacity of 1 mAh/cm<sup>2</sup> at −20 °C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/asset/images/large/am2c10915_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c10915&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Considering the critical role of SEI chemistry in the Na deposition behavior, the X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) depth analysis was further conducted on the cycled Na metal electrodes with different electrolytes. After cycled with the 1.0-E/D<sub>C</sub> electrolyte at 25 °C, peaks of C–C, C–O, and C═O exhibited sharp signals at the Na metal electrode surface (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/suppl_file/am2c10915_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>), representing the abundant organic composites. However, the organic-rich SEI has been reported to be lacking in passivation ability, which cannot effectively prevent the Na metal electrode from continuous reactions with the electrolytes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> Instead, when we used the 0.3-D (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/suppl_file/am2c10915_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>) or 0.3-D/T electrolyte (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a), NaF and Na<sub>2</sub>O species prevailed with sharp peaks, suggestive of the inorganic-dominant SEI on the cycled Na metal electrode. When the temperature was decreased to −20 °C, interestingly, SEI induced in these three electrolytes was detected with more intense Na Auger signals. This indicated that a thinner SEI layer was formed at LT, which may be attributed to the depressed parasitic reactions as the temperature decreases. To investigate the effect of adding THF, we also compared the SEI chemistry formed with the 0.3-D and 0.3-D/T electrolytes. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 5</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/suppl_file/am2c10915_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S11</a>, the SEI formed in 0.3-D/T displayed reduced NaF intensity but more oligomers and flexible alkyl carbonates, implying that the free THF molecules led to a more solvent-derived SEI. The enriched organic components residing in the SEI offered sufficient Na<sup>+</sup> transportation, thus facilitating the LT kinetics. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref31" aria-label="reference 27 and 31">(27,31)</a></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/asset/images/medium/am2c10915_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/asset/images/large/am2c10915_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. C 1s, O 1s, and F 1s XPS spectra of Na metal cycled for 10 cycles in the 0.3-D/T electrolyte at (a) 25 °C and (b) −20 °C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/asset/images/large/am2c10915_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c10915&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Encouraged by the superior kinetics of the designed dilute electrolyte, Na/Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO4)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) full cells were assembled to evaluate the practicability. When cycling at 25 °C, the full cells with the 0.3-D and 0.3-D/T electrolytes were found to retain similarly stable capacity output after 400 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a), which can be ascribed to the favorable Na compatibility of the ether solvents. Conversely, a linear capacity decay occurred with the 1.0-E/D<sub>C</sub> counterpart, indicating a continuous Na consumption related to the nonprotective SEI. The LT electrochemical performances of full cells coupling with different electrolytes were further probed. With the conventional 1.0-E/D<sub>C</sub> system, full cells could only afford a capacity of ∼47 mAh/g after 20 cycles at −20 °C, which was related to its enormous impedance. The full cell using the 0.3-D electrolyte retained a capacity of ∼60 mAh/g but encountered a drastic fluctuation with the cycles proceeding to 100 cycles. In comparison, a high discharge capacity of 80 mAh/g and minimal capacity degradation over 250 cycles were realized in the 0.3-D/T system (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b). Regarding the voltage profiles of the cells cycling at −20 °C, the charge plateaus in 0.3-D and 1.0-E/D<sub>C</sub> systems both experienced severe polarization compared to those at room temperature (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 6</a>c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/suppl_file/am2c10915_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S12</a>) due to the huge kinetic barrier. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> It is also noticed that the charging voltage curves became more sloping than the discharging ones, implying that the performance loss could be more attributed to the hindered Na plating process. In sharp contrast, the cell employing 0.3-D/T electrolyte displayed a restricted polarization, along with a longer charge plateau (∼3.42 V) and a higher discharge plateau (∼3.33 V), indicating the enhanced reaction kinetics. Hence, speedy Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics enabled by 0.3-D/T electrolyte can effectively boost the rate and LT performances plus extend the life span for NMBs, as the enhanced full cells electrochemical performances reveal.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/asset/images/medium/am2c10915_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/asset/images/large/am2c10915_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Long cycle performance of Na/NVP cells with different electrolytes cycling at the current density of 1 C (1 C = 105 mA/g) at 25 °C. (b) LT (−20 °C) cycle performance of Na/NVP cells with various electrolytes. (c) Voltage profiles of the Na/NVP cells cycling at −20 °C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/asset/images/large/am2c10915_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c10915&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In conclusion, we developed a DEGDME-based electrolyte with low viscosity and weakened solvation effect of Na<sup>+</sup>/solvent by lowering the salt concentration and introducing THF cosolvent. A solvent-derived SEI layer was constructed due to the increased free solvents in dilute electrolyte and guaranteed adequate Na<sup>+</sup> transport through SEI at LT, as the MD simulation and XPS analysis unveiled. The as-designed electrolyte decreased the Na/Na symmetric cell impedance from &gt;4000 Ω to only ∼10 Ω at −20 °C due to the reduced activation energies for Na<sup>+</sup> ion desolvation and transport process. Such accelerated reaction kinetics enabled the dendrite-free Na deposition, further spurring remarkable cycle stability for 1000 h with a low overpotential of ∼0.01 V. As-constructed Na/NVP full cells retained a discharge capacity of ∼80 mAh/g and a limited polarization (∼0.1 V) at −20 °C. The present strategy, therefore, represents a promising approach to LT applications of NMBs.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Materials Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Cell-grade NaPF<sub>6</sub>, DEGDME, THF, EC, and DEC were purchased from Dodo Chem Co., Ltd., for the electrolyte preparation. Preparation of the electrolytes was conducted in the glovebox filled with high-purity argon. NVP powder was synthesized by the solid-state method with Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>, V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, and glucose (C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>12</sub>O<sub>6</sub>) as raw materials. Glucose was used as a carbon source, and its weight ratio to other raw materials was 1:5. The raw materials weighed in stoichiometric ratio were ball-milled for 6 h and then dried to obtain the precursors. Afterward, the precursors were sintered in an argon furnace at 800 °C for 10 h to obtain NVP@C powder samples.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Calculation Methods</h3><div class="NLM_p last">MD simulations of the electrolyte systems were performed by employing the optimized potentials for liquid-simulations all-atom (OPLS-AA) force field in the Gromacs program suite. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> The detailed calculation methods are provided in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/suppl_file/am2c10915_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The viscosity and ionic conductivity of the electrolytes at −40 to 25 °C were tested with a HAAKE MCR302 rotational rheometer and Leici DDS-307 conductivity measuring meter, respectively. The wettability of electrolytes was conducted by recording their contact angles on the PP separators on a DataPhysics OCA50AF. SEM images were obtained from a Zeiss Sigma 300VP. XPS characterizations were conducted on a Thermo Fisher Scientific ESCALAB 250Xi. During both SEM and XPS tests, air-sensitive samples were stored and transferred in an argon atmosphere before being sent into the test chamber, and all the retrieved electrodes were rinsed with DEGDME and dried in a vacuum environment.</div></div><div id="sec4_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The EIS tests of the Na/Na symmetric cells were performed with a frequency range of 100 mHz to 10<sup>3</sup> kHz by using the multichannel workstation (Biologic VMP-3). Na/Cu cells were assembled to analyze the nucleation overpotentials. The galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCV) tests for the cycling behavior of the Na/Na cells and Na/NVP cells were tested on a Neware CT-4008T.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c10915?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c10915</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Detailed calculation methods and Figures S1–S12 (including photographs, voltage profiles, results of MD simulation, Nyquist plots, and XPS spectra) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/suppl_file/am2c10915_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c10915/suppl_file/am2c10915_si_001.pdf">am2c10915_si_001.pdf (1.0 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.2c10915" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Electrode processing plays an important role in advancing lithium-ion battery technologies and has a significant impact on cell energy d., manufg. cost, and throughput.  Compared to the extensive research on materials development, however, there has been much less effort in this area.  In this Review, we outline each step in the electrode processing of lithium-ion batteries from materials to cell assembly, summarize the recent progress in individual steps, deconvolute the interplays between those steps, discuss the underlying constraints, and share some prospective technologies.  This Review aims to provide an overview of the whole process in lithium-ion battery fabrication from powder to cell formation and bridge the gap between academic development and industrial manufg.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo3elulR4-xsrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgS4XOXY_4w_w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXitlaju7fO&amp;md5=eb4a00516ca789ed96878971df43e9f0</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201701428"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, W.-B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.-K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S.-X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium-Ion Batteries: From Academic Research to Practical Commercialization</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Li-ion battery commercialized by Sony in 1991 has the highest energy-d. among practical rechargeable batteries and is widely used in electronic devices, elec. vehicles, and stationary energy storage system in the world.  Moreover, the battery market is rapidly growing in the world and further fast-growing is expected.  With expansion of the demand and applications, price of lithium and cobalt resources is increasing.  We are, therefore, motivated to study Na- and K-ion batteries for stationary energy storage system because of much abundant Na and K resources and the wide distribution in the world.  In this account, we review developments of Na- and K-ion batteries with mainly introducing our previous and present researches in comparison to that of Li-ion battery.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoSYlvjIhnmwLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgS4XOXY_4w_w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXivFClsL0%253D&amp;md5=e037a6aa3e09603bd67ac8e676fcea76</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201800079"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chayambuka, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mulder, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Danilov, D.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Cell Press</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Considering the limited energy d. of traditional graphite-anode-based lithium (Li)-ion batteries, alternative high-capacity anodes are greatly needed for the next-generation high-energy-d. battery systems.  In this regard, Li metal is well known to be one of the most promising anodes due to its ultrahigh capacity (3,860 mAh g-1) and the very low std. neg. electrochem. potential (-3.040 V).  However, dendrite growth and high reactivity of Li metal anodes result in low cycling efficiency and severe safety concerns.  The recent revival of research and development on Li metal anodes has brought new, in-depth understanding and key exptl. progress toward Li metal protection and enhanced performance of Li metal batteries.  In this Perspective, we concisely review recent discoveries in this field and suggest possible new research directions for further development of Li metal batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq2VJIgeaFlXbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgS4XOXY_4w_w"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Composite lithium metal anode by melt infusion of lithium into a 3D conducting scaffold with lithiophilic coating</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liang, Zheng; Lin, Dingchang; Zhao, Jie; Lu, Zhenda; Liu, Yayuan; Liu, Chong; Lu, Yingying; Wang, Haotian; Yan, Kai; Tao, Xinyong; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">National Academy of Sciences</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium metal-based battery is considered one of the best energy storage systems due to its high theor. capacity and lowest anode potential of all.  However, dendritic growth and virtually relative infinity vol. change during long-term cycling often lead to severe safety hazards and catastrophic failure.  Here, a stable lithium-scaffold composite electrode is developed by lithium melt infusion into a 3D porous carbon matrix with "lithiophilic" coating.  Lithium is uniformly entrapped on the matrix surface and in the 3D structure.  The resulting composite electrode possesses a high conductive surface area and excellent structural stability upon galvanostatic cycling.  We showed stable cycling of this composite electrode with small Li plating/stripping overpotential (&lt;90 mV) at a high c.d. of 3 mA/cm2 over 80 cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGot06M-5acQyrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljV_9lB_N_l8g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xjt12iuro%253D&amp;md5=61d807b5bd719f70487b4cfba2b8933d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b03071"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Matios, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Critical Role of Ultrathin Graphene Films with Tunable Thickness in Enabling Highly Stable Sodium Metal Anodes</span>. <i>Nano Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>17</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">11</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">6808</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">6815</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
