                        cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Critical Role of Ultrathin Graphene Films with Tunable Thickness in Enabling Highly Stable Sodium Metal Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Huan; Wang, Chuanlong; Matios, Edward; Li, Weiyang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">17</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6808-6815</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NALEFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1530-6984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na metal showed great promise as an anode material for the next-generation energy storage systems because of its high theor. capacity, low cost, and high earth abundance.  However, the extremely high reactivity of Na metal with org. electrolyte gives unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) and growth of Na dendrites upon repeated electrochem. stripping/plating, causing poor cycling performance, and serious safety issues.  Herein, the authors present highly stable and dendrite-free Na metal anodes over a wide current range and long-term cycling via directly applying free-standing graphene films with tunable thickness on Na metal surface.  The authors systematically study the dependence of Na anode stability on the thickness of the graphene film at different current densities and capacities.  Only a few nanometer (∼2-3 nm) differences in the graphene thickness can have decisive influence on the stability and rate capability of Na anodes.  To achieve the optimal performance, the thickness of the graphene film covered on Na surface needs to be meticulously selected based on the applied c.d.  With a multilayer graphene film (∼5 nm in thickness) as a protective layer, stable Na cycling behavior was 1st achieved in carbonate electrolyte without any additives over 100 cycles at a c.d. ≤2 mA/cm2 with a high capacity of 3 mA h/cm2.  The authors believe the work could be a viable route toward high-energy Na battery systems, and can provide valuable insights into the Li batteries as well.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoKQT_0ZsM4qrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljV_9lB_N_l8g"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Developments and Perspectives on Emerging High-Energy-Density Sodium-Metal Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Yunxiao; Wang, Yanxia; Wang, Yun-Xia; Feng, Xiangming; Chen, Weihua; Ai, Xinping; Yang, Hanxi; Cao, Yuliang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chem</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Cell Press</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Emerging rechargeable sodium-metal batteries (SMBs) are gaining extensive attention because of the high energy d., low cost, and promising potentials for large-scale applications.  The mechanism investigation and performance optimization of SMBs are of great significance for fundamental science and practical applications.  Consequently, this review provides fundamental insights into the cell chem. and recent progress on several representative SMBs, including Na-O2, Na-CO2, Na-SO2, and room-temp. Na-S batteries, for which the Na-storage mechanisms, potential solns. for enhancing battery performance, and future perspectives are discussed.  We emphasize the importance and challenges of sodium-metal anodes, as well as summarize and highlight feasible strategies to address the challenging issues facing them.  Combined with current research achievements, this review offers future research directions from the viewpoint of better SMB full cells regarding cathode design and anode protection with compatible electrolyte systems.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqB194LD5n6R7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljV_9lB_N_l8g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXhvFKrsL%252FO&amp;md5=d52a93c546799aab67072f7a99908622</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201605512"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Choudhury, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nath, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Archer, L.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Toward a Stable Sodium Metal Anode in Carbonate Electrolyte: A Compact, Inorganic Alloy Interface</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zheng, Xueying; Fu, Haoyu; Hu, Chenchen; Xu, Hui; Huang, Ying; Wen, Jiayun; Sun, Huabin; Luo, Wei; Huang, Yunhui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">707-714</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPCLCD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1948-7185</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Development of the next-generation, high-energy-d., low-cost batteries will likely be fueled by sodium (Na) metal batteries because of their high capacity and the abundance of Na.  However, their practical application is significantly plagued by the hyper-reactivity of Na metal, unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI), and dendritic Na growth, leading to continuous electrolyte decompn., low Coulombic efficiency, large impedance, and safety concerns.  Herein, we add a small amt. of SnCl2 additive in a common carbonate electrolyte so that the spontaneous reaction between SnCl2 and Na metal enables in situ formation of a Na-Sn alloy layer and a compact NaCl-rich SEI.  Benefitting from this design, rapid interfacial ion transfer is realized and direct exposure of Na metal to the electrolyte is prohibited, which jointly achieve a nondendritic deposition morphol. and a markedly reduced voltage hysteresis in a Na/Na sym. cell for over 500 h.  The Na/SnCl2-added electrolyte/Na3V2(PO4)3 full cell exhibits high capacity retention over cycling and excellent rate capability (101 mAh/g at 10 C).  This work can provide an easily scalable and cost-effective approach for developing high-performance Na-metal batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGojq3lBHloW1rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljV_9lB_N_l8g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXitFGgtLw%253D&amp;md5=f4944af431abe48177ff302656a7d1c9</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemrev.8b00642"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Paek, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mitlin, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, S.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium metal anodes: Emerging solutions to dendrite growth</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lee, Byeongyong; Paek, Eunsu; Mitlin, David; Lee, Seung Woo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Reviews (Washington, DC, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">119</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5416-5460</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CHREAY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0009-2665</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">This comprehensive Review focuses on the key challenges and recent progress regarding sodium-metal anodes employed in sodium-metal batteries (SMBs).  The metal anode is the essential component of emerging energy storage systems such as sodium sulfur and sodium selenium, which are discussed as example full-cell applications.  We begin with a description of the differences in the chem. and phys. properties of Na metal vs. the oft-studied Li metal, and a corresponding discussion regarding the no. of ways in which Na does not follow Li-inherited paradigms in its electrochem. behavior.  We detail the major challenges for Na-metal systems that at this time limit the feasibility of SMBs.  The core Na anode problems are the following interrelated degrdn. mechanisms: An unstable solid electrolyte interphase with most org. electrolytes, "mossy" and "lath-like" metal dendrite growth for liq. systems, poor Coulombic efficiency, and gas evolution.  Even solid-state Na batteries are not immune, with metal dendrites being reported.  The solns. may be subdivided into the following interrelated taxonomy: Improved electrolytes and electrolyte additives tailored for Na-metal anodes, interfacial engineering between the metal and the liq. or solid electrolyte, electrode architectures that both reduce the c.d. during plating-stripping and serve as effective hosts that shield the Na metal from excessive reactions, and alloy design to tune the bulk properties of the metal per se.  For instance, stable plating-stripping of Na is extremely difficult with conventional carbonate solvents but has been reported with ethers and glymes.  Solid-state electrolytes (SSEs) such as beta-alumina solid electrolyte (BASE), sodium superionic conductor (NASICON), and sodium thiophosphate (75Na2S·25P2S5) present highly exciting opportunities for SMBs that avoid the dangers of flammable liqs.  Even SSEs are not immune to dendrites, however, which grow through the defects in the bulk pellet, but may be controlled through interfacial energy modification.  We conclude with a discussion of the key research areas that we feel are the most fruitful for further pursuit.  In our opinion, greatly improved understanding and control of the SEI structure is the key to cycling stability.  A holistic approach involving complementary post-mortem, in situ, and operando analyses to elucidate full battery cell level structure-performance relations is advocated.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqQ1buYt3eMx7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljV_9lB_N_l8g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXmsFCrtL4%253D&amp;md5=50399f6bff0463f55ea84f50d36fb802</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1126/science.aam6014"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pei, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Joubert, L.-M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chin, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Koh, A.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Cryogenic transmission electron microscopy (cryo-TEM) is a valuable tool recently proposed to investigate battery electrodes.  Despite being employed for Li-based battery materials, cryo-TEM measurements for Na-based electrochem. energy storage systems are not commonly reported.  In particular, elucidating the chem. and morphol. behavior of the Na-metal electrode in contact with a non-aq. liq. electrolyte soln. could provide useful insights that may lead to a better understanding of metal cells during operation.  Here, using cryo-TEM, we investigate the effect of fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) additive on the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) structure of a Na-metal electrode.  Without FEC, the NaPF6-contg. carbonate-based electrolyte reacts with the metal electrode to produce an unstable SEI, rich in Na2CO3 and Na3PO4, which constantly consumes the sodium reservoir of the cell during cycling.  When FEC is used, the Na-metal electrode forms a multilayer SEI structure comprising an outer NaF-rich amorphous phase and an inner Na3PO4 phase.  This layered structure stabilizes the SEI and prevents further reactions between the electrolyte and the Na metal.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpOPqkFE4vvLrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lic9nO2lQ-EDA"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In Situ Construction of a LiF-Enriched Interface for Stable All-Solid-State Batteries and its Origin Revealed by Cryo-TEM</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sheng, Ouwei; Zheng, Jianhui; Ju, Zhijin; Jin, Chengbin; Wang, Yao; Chen, Mei; Nai, Jianwei; Liu, Tiefeng; Zhang, Wenkui; Liu, Yujing; Tao, Xinyong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The application of solid polymer electrolytes (SPEs) is still inherently limited by the unstable lithium (Li)/electrolyte interface, despite the advantages of security, flexibility, and workability of SPEs.  Herein, the lithium(Li)/electrolyte interface is modified by introducing Li2S additive to harvest stable all-solid-state lithium metal batteries (LMBs).  Cryo-transmission electron microscopy (cryo-TEM) results demonstrate a mosaic interface between poly(ethylene oxide) (PEO) electrolytes and Li metal anodes, in which abundant cryst. grains of Li, Li2O, lithium hydroxide, and Li2CO3 are randomly distributed.  Besides, cryo-TEM visualization, combined with mol. dynamics simulations, reveals that the introduction of Li2S accelerates the decompn. of N(CF3SO2)2- and consequently promotes the formation of abundant LiF nanocrystals in the Li/PEO interface.  The generated LiF is further verified to inhibit the breakage of C-O bonds in the polymer chains and prevents the continuous interface reaction between Li and PEO.  Therefore, the all-solid-state LMBs with the lithium fluoride(LiF)-enriched interface exhibit improved cycling capability and stability in a cell configuration with an ultralong lifespan over 1800 h.  This work is believed to open up a new avenue for rational design of high-performance all-solid-state LMBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq_jNCn8D7cQrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lic9nO2lQ-EDA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhtlyjt73F&amp;md5=c6f97d1a4f540f49512a32cb08253002</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1021/acscentsci.5b00328"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Seh, Z.                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Highly Reversible Room-Temperature Sodium Metal Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Seh, Zhi Wei; Sun, Jie; Sun, Yongming; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Central Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">449-455</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACSCII</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Owing to its low cost and high natural abundance, sodium metal is among the most promising anode materials for energy storage technologies beyond lithium ion batteries.  However, room-temp. sodium metal anodes suffer from poor reversibility during long-term plating and stripping, mainly due to formation of nonuniform solid electrolyte interphase as well as dendritic growth of sodium metal.  Herein it is reported for the first time that a simple liq. electrolyte, sodium hexafluorophosphate in glymes (mono-, di-, and tetraglyme), can enable highly reversible and nondendritic plating-stripping of sodium metal anodes at room temp.  High av. Coulombic efficiencies of 99.9% were achieved over 300 plating-stripping cycles at 0.5 mA cm-2.  The long-term reversibility was found to arise from the formation of a uniform, inorg. solid electrolyte interphase made of sodium oxide and sodium fluoride, which is highly impermeable to electrolyte solvent and conducive to nondendritic growth.  As a proof of concept, a room-temp. sodium-sulfur battery is demonstrated using this class of electrolytes, paving the way for the development of next-generation, sodium-based energy storage technologies.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrdqXOWzEW74bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lic9nO2lQ-EDA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhvVGmtrfL&amp;md5=6155e3c781a24b8340081c2ad4444616</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2016.09.013"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mishra, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qian, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Engelhard, M.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Enabling room temperature sodium metal batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cao, Ruiguo; Mishra, Kuber; Li, Xiaolin; Qian, Jiangfeng; Engelhard, Mark H.; Bowden, Mark E.; Han, Kee Sung; Mueller, Karl T.; Henderson, Wesley A.; Zhang, Ji-Guang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable batteries based upon sodium (Na+) cations are at the core of many new battery chemistries beyond Li-ion batteries.  Rather than using carbon or alloy-based anodes, the direct utilization of solid sodium metal as an anode would be highly advantageous, but its use has been highly problematic due to its high reactivity.  In this work, it is demonstrated that, by tailoring the electrolyte formulation, solid Na metal can be electrochem. plated/stripped at ambient temp. with high efficiency (&gt;99%) on both copper and inexpensive aluminum current collectors thereby enabling a shift in focus to new battery chem. couples based upon Na metal operating at ambient temp.  These highly concd. electrolytes have enabled excellent cycling stability of Na metal batteries using Na metal anode and Na3V2(PO4)3 cathode at high rates with very high efficiency.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGojzkibwdBOJLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljurH03GiMoFw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXotlKisg%253D%253D&amp;md5=c9d0b62dd81e74bd6fbd501f5ba43272</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201803049"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhong, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-Performance Sodium Metal Anodes Enabled by a Bifunctional Potassium Salt</span>. <i>Angew.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-Performance Sodium Metal Anodes Enabled by a Bifunctional Potassium Salt</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Shi, Qiuwei; Zhong, Yiren; Wu, Min; Wang, Hongzhi; Wang, Hailiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Developing Na metal anodes that can be deeply cycled with high efficiency for a long time is a prerequisite for rechargeable Na metal batteries to be practically useful despite their notable advantages in theor. energy d. and potential low cost.  Their high chem. reactivity with the electrolyte and tendency for dendrite formation are two major issues limiting the reversibility of Na metal electrodes.  In this work, we introduce for the first time potassium bis(trifluoromethylsulfonyl)imide (KTFSI) as a bifunctional electrolyte additive to stabilize Na metal electrodes, in which the TFSI- anions decomp. into lithium nitride and oxynitrides to render a desirable solid electrolyte interphase layer while the K+ cations preferentially adsorb onto Na protrusions and provide electrostatic shielding to suppress dendritic deposition.  Through the cooperation of the cations and anions, we have realized Na metal electrodes that can be deeply cycled at a capacity of 10 mAh cm-2 for hundreds of hours.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpKftAh-fRpkLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljurH03GiMoFw"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Low-temperature characterization of lithium-ion carbon anodes via microperturbation measurement</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Chunsheng; Appleby, A. John; Little, Frank E.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The low-temp. performance limits of Johnson Matthey (JM) 287 graphite and mesocarbon microbead (MCMB) 6-10 coke were investigated using galvanostatic intermittent titrn. (GITT) and electrochem. impedance spectroscopy.  The poor low-temp. (-30°C) performance of graphite insertion anodes results from a low lithium insertion capacity because polarization or overpotential is higher than the stage transformation plateau potential.  This results in a shorter plateau potential region contg. the lithium-rich stages, e.g., Li0.33C6, Li0.5C6, and Li1C6.  Overall, there is an incomplete transformation from Li-poor to Li-rich stages when the cutoff potential is limited to 0.0 V (vs. Li/Li+) to avoid metallic lithium deposition.  The good low-temp. performance of MCMB 6-10 coke is attributed to the smooth change of equil. Li content as a function of potential.  The high polarization only decreases Li insertion capacity by a small percentage.  At room temp., stage transformation is the rate-controlling step of electrochem. Li insertion-extn. kinetics for JM 287 graphite.  However, at -30°C the resistance of solid electrolyte interphase film increases by a factor of &gt;27, and becomes limiting.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqgbACNmXNAarVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljurH03GiMoFw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD38XltVGls7s%253D&amp;md5=87ee2d2826e98941cf9710b7b7cf24c1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit22" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2003.10.016"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, S.     <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1057-1061</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ELCAAV</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Science B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Cycling performance of Li-ion batteries was studied by using electrochem. impedance spectroscopy.  Results showed that total resistance of the Li-ion batteries is mainly composed of bulk resistance (Rb), solid-state interface resistance (Rsei) and charge-transfer resistance (Rct).  During cycling, the Rb and Rsei do not change while the Rct displays two min. in the same voltage regions where the major peaks of differential capacities are present.  The Rct can be linked to kinetics of the battery electrochem. reaction.  In response to the temp. change, the Rb and Rsei vary in a very similar manner, while the Rct shows significant difference.  In the fully charged and discharged states as well as at the low temps. (≤20°), the total resistance of the Li-ion batteries is predominated by the Rct.  Using the term of the Rct, we explained two low-temp. phenomena of the Li-ion battery: (a) charging of a fully discharged battery is much more difficult than discharging of a fully charged battery, and (b) both the power (operating voltage) and energy (delivered capacity) are substantially reduced.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGplYUwg57Nby7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljurH03GiMoFw"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Materials insights into low-temperature performances of lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhu, Gaolong; Wen, Kechun; Lv, Weiqiang; Zhou, Xingzhi; Liang, Yachun; Yang, Fei; Chen, Zhilin; Zou, Minda; Li, Jinchao; Zhang, Yuqian; He, Weidong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Li-ion batteries (LIBs) have been employed in many fields including cell phones, laptop computers, elec. vehicles (EVs) and stationary energy storage wells due to their high energy d. and pronounced recharge ability.  However, energy and power capabilities of LIBs decrease sharply at low operation temps.  In particular, the charge process becomes extremely sluggish at temps. &lt;-20°, which severely limits the applications of LIBs in some cold areas during winter.  Extensive research showed that the electrolyte/electrode compn. and microstructure are of fundamental importance to low-temp. performances of LIBs.  The authors review the recent findings in the role of electrolytes, anodes, and cathodes in the low temp. performances of LIBs.  The overview aims to understand comprehensively the fundamental origin of low-temp. performances of LIBs from a materials perspective and facilitates the development of high-performance Li-ion battery materials that are operational at a large range of working temps.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGopWzL9s8vIwbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lipkFlIq7POMw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhsFeht7vE&amp;md5=1103aa2d337f63b5eef9bd2899a0973c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202001440"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Lithium Difluorophosphate-Based Dual-Salt Low Concentration Electrolytes for Lithium Metal Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lithium Difluorophosphate-Based Dual-Salt Low Concentration Electrolytes for Lithium Metal Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zheng, Hao; Xiang, Hongfa; Jiang, Fuyang; Liu, Yongchao; Sun, Yi; Liang, Xin; Feng, Yuezhan; Yu, Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The safety hazards and low Coulombic efficiency originating from the growth of lithium dendrites and decompn. of the electrolyte restrict the practical application of Li metal batteries (LMBs).  Inspired by the low cost of low concn. electrolytes (LCEs) in industrial applications, dual-salt LCEs employing 0.1 M Li difluorophosphate (LiDFP) and 0.4 M LiBOB/LiFSI/LiTFSI are proposed to construct a robust and conductive interphase on a Li metal anode.  Compared with the conventional electrolyte using 1 M LiPF6, the ionic cond. of LCEs is reduced but the cond. decrement of the separator immersed in LCEs is moderate, esp. for the LiDFP-LiFSI and LiDFP-LiTFSI electrolytes.  The accurate Coulombic efficiency (CE) of the Li||Cu cells increases from 83.3% (electrolyte using 1 M LiPF6) to 97.6%, 94.5%, and 93.6% for LiDFP-LiBOB, LiDFP-LiFSI, and LiDFP-LiTFSI electrolytes, resp.  The capacity retention of Li||LiFePO4 cells using the LiDFP-LiBOB electrolyte reaches 95.4% along with a CE over 99.8% after 300 cycles at a c.d. of 2.0 mA cm-2 and the capacity reaches 103.7 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of up to 16.0 mA cm-2.  This work provides a dual-salt LCE for practical LMBs and presents a new perspective for the design of electrolytes for LMBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr_0CCahCFtabVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lipkFlIq7POMw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhtF2ltbnI&amp;md5=c7adb92cf06217227ffafaf67b76b9b3</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b14878"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, S.-M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Choi, J.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kwon, M.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, K. Using this electrolyte, a very high Coulombic efficiency of 99.3% at the 120th cycle for Na plating/stripping is obtained in Na/stainless steel (SS) cells with highly reduced corrosivity toward Na metal and high oxidn. durability (over 4.9 V vs. Na/Na+) without corrosion of the aluminum cathode current collector.                         cas_cit26R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">26</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Extremely Stable Sodium Metal Batteries Enabled by Localized High-Concentration Electrolytes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zheng, Jianming; Chen, Shuru; Zhao, Wengao; Song, Junhua; Engelhard, Mark H.; Zhang, Ji-Guang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Energy Letters</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium (Na) metal is a promising anode for Na-ion batteries.  However, the high reactivity of Na metal with electrolytes and the low Na metal cycling efficiency have limited its practical application in rechargeable Na metal batteries.  High-concn. electrolytes (HCE, ≥4 M) consisting of sodium bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide (NaFSI) and ether solvent could ensure the stable cycling of Na metal with high Coulombic efficiency but at the cost of high viscosity, poor wettability, and high salt cost.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Efforts were made to differentiate the contributions to the so-called ion transfer barrier at the electrolyte/graphite junction from 2 distinct processes: (1) desolvation of Li+ before it enters graphene interlayer and (2) the subsequent migration of bare Li+ through the ad hoc interphase.  By leveraging a scenario where no substantial interphase was formed on Li+ intercalation hosts, the authors were able to quantify the distribution of ion transfer activation energy between these 2 interfacial processes and hence identify the desolvation process of Li+ as the major energy-consuming step.  The result confirmed the earlier belief that the rate-detg. step in the charging of a graphitic anode in Li+ intercalation chem. relates to the stripping of solvation sheath of Li+, which is closely interwoven with the interphasial resistance to Li+ migration.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpe14kaW97iRbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljfgEXWBIfjtA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3cXlslyhsbs%253D&amp;md5=a9ccc3e2ccf3ecece5b04e29224c48a4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit29" data-doi="10.1039/D0CS00033G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Matios, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Combining Theories and Experiments to Understand the Sodium Nucleation Behavior towards Safe Sodium Metal Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Rechargeable sodium (Na) based batteries have gained tremendous research interest because of the high natural abundance and low cost of Na resources, as well as electrochem. similarities with lithium (Li) based batteries.  However, despite the great potential as a candidate for next-generation grid-scale energy storage, the implementation of the Na metal anode has been primarily hindered by dendritic and "dead" Na formation that leads to low Coulombic efficiency, short lifespan and even safety concerns.  Na dendrite formation mainly originates from the uncontrolled Na deposition behavior in the absence of nucleation site regulation.  Hence, the Na nucleation and initial stage of growth are critically important for the final morphol. of Na metal.  Here, this tutorial review aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of the importance of the nucleation behavior towards dendrite-free Na metal anodes.  Firstly, we start with an introduction about the advantages of Na metal batteries over the Li counterpart and the challenges faced by Na metal anodes.  The differences between metallic Li and Na are summarized according to advanced in situ characterization techniques.  Next, we elucidate the key factors that influence the Na nucleation and growth behaviors based on the existing theor. models.  Then, we review the state-of-the-art approaches that have been applied to effectively regulate Na nucleation for dendrite-free Na deposition.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) power virtually all modern portable devices and elec. vehicles, and their ubiquity continues to grow.  With increasing applications, however, come increasing challenges, esp. when operating conditions deviate from room temp.  While high-temp. performance and degrdn. has been extensively studied in LIBs, sub-zero Celsius performance has received less attention, despite being crit. for batteries in transportation roles.  Although many individual processes contribute to the capacity loss commonly obsd. in LIBs at low temps., most of them are governed to some extent by the non-aq. liq. electrolyte present throughout the cell interior.  Therefore, electrolyte engineering presents an unparalleled opportunity to study and address the fundamental causes of low-temp. failure.  In this review, we first briefly cover the various processes that det. lithium-ion performance below 0°C.  Then, we outline recent literature on electrolyte-based strategies to improve said performance, including various additives, solvents and lithium salts.  Finally, we summarize these findings and provide some perspectives on the current state of the field, including promising new areas of investigation.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpR3IYuj2WWH7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljbvGX-SH7DYQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB38XhtFOlur8%253D&amp;md5=8c492936775080d684965a092667550e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit33" data-doi="10.1016/j.softx.2015.06.001"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Abraham, M. Electrode processing plays an important role in advancing lithium-ion battery technologies and has a significant impact on cell energy d., manufg. cost, and throughput.  Compared to the extensive research on materials development, however, there has been much less effort in this area.  In this Review, we outline each step in the electrode processing of lithium-ion batteries from materials to cell assembly, summarize the recent progress in individual steps, deconvolute the interplays between those steps, discuss the underlying constraints, and share some prospective technologies. With expansion of the demand and applications, price of lithium and cobalt resources is increasing.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Considering the limited energy d. of traditional graphite-anode-based lithium (Li)-ion batteries, alternative high-capacity anodes are greatly needed for the next-generation high-energy-d. battery systems.  In this regard, Li metal is well known to be one of the most promising anodes due to its ultrahigh capacity (3,860 mAh g-1) and the very low std. neg. electrochem. potential (-3.040 V).  However, dendrite growth and high reactivity of Li metal anodes result in low cycling efficiency and severe safety concerns.  The recent revival of research and development on Li metal anodes has brought new, in-depth understanding and key exptl. progress toward Li metal protection and enhanced performance of Li metal batteries.  In this Perspective, we concisely review recent discoveries in this field and suggest possible new research directions for further development of Li metal batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq2VJIgeaFlXbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj18vZvaD_Xiw"> &gt;&gt;             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">National Academy of Sciences</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium metal-based battery is considered one of the best energy storage systems due to its high theor. capacity and lowest anode potential of all.  However, dendritic growth and virtually relative infinity vol. change during long-term cycling often lead to severe safety hazards and catastrophic failure.  Here, a stable lithium-scaffold composite electrode is developed by lithium melt infusion into a 3D porous carbon matrix with "lithiophilic" coating.  Lithium is uniformly entrapped on the matrix surface and in the 3D structure.  The resulting composite electrode possesses a high conductive surface area and excellent structural stability upon galvanostatic cycling.  We showed stable cycling of this composite electrode with small Li plating/stripping overpotential (&lt;90 mV) at a high c.d. of 3 mA/cm2 over 80 cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGot06M-5acQyrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj18vZvaD_Xiw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xjt12iuro%253D&amp;md5=61d807b5bd719f70487b4cfba2b8933d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref8" class="rightTabRefNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit8" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b03071"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Matios, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Critical Role of Ultrathin Graphene Films with Tunable Thickness in Enabling Highly Stable Sodium Metal Anodes</span>. <i>Nano Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>17</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">11</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">6808</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">6815</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        rightTab-cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Critical Role of Ultrathin Graphene Films with Tunable Thickness in Enabling Highly Stable Sodium Metal Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Huan; Wang, Chuanlong; Matios, Edward; Li, Weiyang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na metal showed great promise as an anode material for the next-generation energy storage systems because of its high theor. capacity, low cost, and high earth abundance.  However, the extremely high reactivity of Na metal with org. electrolyte gives unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) and growth of Na dendrites upon repeated electrochem. stripping/plating, causing poor cycling performance, and serious safety issues.  Herein, the authors present highly stable and dendrite-free Na metal anodes over a wide current range and long-term cycling via directly applying free-standing graphene films with tunable thickness on Na metal surface.  The authors systematically study the dependence of Na anode stability on the thickness of the graphene film at different current densities and capacities.  Only a few nanometer (∼2-3 nm) differences in the graphene thickness can have decisive influence on the stability and rate capability of Na anodes.  To achieve the optimal performance, the thickness of the graphene film covered on Na surface needs to be meticulously selected based on the applied c.d.  With a multilayer graphene film (∼5 nm in thickness) as a protective layer, stable Na cycling behavior was 1st achieved in carbonate electrolyte without any additives over 100 cycles at a c.d. ≤2 mA/cm2 with a high capacity of 3 mA h/cm2.  The authors believe the work could be a viable route toward high-energy Na battery systems, and can provide valuable insights into the Li batteries as well.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoKQT_0ZsM4qrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj18vZvaD_Xiw"> &gt;&gt;         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Emerging rechargeable sodium-metal batteries (SMBs) are gaining extensive attention because of the high energy d., low cost, and promising potentials for large-scale applications.  The mechanism investigation and performance optimization of SMBs are of great significance for fundamental science and practical applications.  Consequently, this review provides fundamental insights into the cell chem. and recent progress on several representative SMBs, including Na-O2, Na-CO2, Na-SO2, and room-temp.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Development of the next-generation, high-energy-d., low-cost batteries will likely be fueled by sodium (Na) metal batteries because of their high capacity and the abundance of Na.  However, their practical application is significantly plagued by the hyper-reactivity of Na metal, unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI), and dendritic Na growth, leading to continuous electrolyte decompn., low Coulombic efficiency, large impedance, and safety concerns.  Herein, we add a small amt. of SnCl2 additive in a common carbonate electrolyte so that the spontaneous reaction between SnCl2 and Na metal enables in situ formation of a Na-Sn alloy layer and a compact NaCl-rich SEI.  Benefitting from this design, rapid interfacial ion transfer is realized and direct exposure of Na metal to the electrolyte is prohibited, which jointly achieve a nondendritic deposition morphol. and a markedly reduced voltage hysteresis in a Na/Na sym. cell for over 500 h.  The Na/SnCl2-added electrolyte/Na3V2(PO4)3 full cell exhibits high capacity retention over cycling and excellent rate capability (101 mAh/g at 10 C).  This work can provide an easily scalable and cost-effective approach for developing high-performance Na-metal batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGojq3lBHloW1rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhfRwww5YoKsg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXitFGgtLw%253D&amp;md5=f4944af431abe48177ff302656a7d1c9</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref12" class="rightTabRefNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit12" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemrev.8b00642"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Paek, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mitlin, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, S.                         rightTab-cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium metal anodes: Emerging solutions to dendrite growth</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lee, Byeongyong; Paek, Eunsu; Mitlin, David; Lee, Seung Woo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Reviews (Washington, DC, United States)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">This comprehensive Review focuses on the key challenges and recent progress regarding sodium-metal anodes employed in sodium-metal batteries (SMBs).  The metal anode is the essential component of emerging energy storage systems such as sodium sulfur and sodium selenium, which are discussed as example full-cell applications.  We begin with a description of the differences in the chem. and phys. properties of Na metal vs. the oft-studied Li metal, and a corresponding discussion regarding the no. of ways in which Na does not follow Li-inherited paradigms in its electrochem. behavior.  We detail the major challenges for Na-metal systems that at this time limit the feasibility of SMBs.  The core Na anode problems are the following interrelated degrdn. mechanisms: An unstable solid electrolyte interphase with most org. electrolytes, "mossy" and "lath-like" metal dendrite growth for liq. systems, poor Coulombic efficiency, and gas evolution.  Even solid-state Na batteries are not immune, with metal dendrites being reported.  The solns. may be subdivided into the following interrelated taxonomy: Improved electrolytes and electrolyte additives tailored for Na-metal anodes, interfacial engineering between the metal and the liq. or solid electrolyte, electrode architectures that both reduce the c.d. during plating-stripping and serve as effective hosts that shield the Na metal from excessive reactions, and alloy design to tune the bulk properties of the metal per se.  For instance, stable plating-stripping of Na is extremely difficult with conventional carbonate solvents but has been reported with ethers and glymes.  Solid-state electrolytes (SSEs) such as beta-alumina solid electrolyte (BASE), sodium superionic conductor (NASICON), and sodium thiophosphate (75Na2S·25P2S5) present highly exciting opportunities for SMBs that avoid the dangers of flammable liqs.  Even SSEs are not immune to dendrites, however, which grow through the defects in the bulk pellet, but may be controlled through interfacial energy modification.  We conclude with a discussion of the key research areas that we feel are the most fruitful for further pursuit.  In our opinion, greatly improved understanding and control of the SEI structure is the key to cycling stability.  A holistic approach involving complementary post-mortem, in situ, and operando analyses to elucidate full battery cell level structure-performance relations is advocated.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqQ1buYt3eMx7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhfRwww5YoKsg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXmsFCrtL4%253D&amp;md5=50399f6bff0463f55ea84f50d36fb802</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref13" class="rightTabRefNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit13" data-doi="10.1126/science.aam6014"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pei, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Joubert, L.-M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chin, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Koh, A.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Whereas std. transmission electron microscopy studies are unable to preserve the native state of chem. reactive and beam-sensitive battery materials after operation, such materials remain pristine at cryogenic conditions.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Cryogenic transmission electron microscopy (cryo-TEM) is a valuable tool recently proposed to investigate battery electrodes.  Despite being employed for Li-based battery materials, cryo-TEM measurements for Na-based electrochem. energy storage systems are not commonly reported.  In particular, elucidating the chem. and morphol. behavior of the Na-metal electrode in contact with a non-aq. liq. electrolyte soln. could provide useful insights that may lead to a better understanding of metal cells during operation.  Here, using cryo-TEM, we investigate the effect of fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) additive on the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) structure of a Na-metal electrode.  Without FEC, the NaPF6-contg. carbonate-based electrolyte reacts with the metal electrode to produce an unstable SEI, rich in Na2CO3 and Na3PO4, which constantly consumes the sodium reservoir of the cell during cycling.  When FEC is used, the Na-metal electrode forms a multilayer SEI structure comprising an outer NaF-rich amorphous phase and an inner Na3PO4 phase.  This layered structure stabilizes the SEI and prevents further reactions between the electrolyte and the Na metal.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpOPqkFE4vvLrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhfRwww5YoKsg"> &gt;&gt;         </div><div class="casAbstract">The application of solid polymer electrolytes (SPEs) is still inherently limited by the unstable lithium (Li)/electrolyte interface, despite the advantages of security, flexibility, and workability of SPEs.  Herein, the lithium(Li)/electrolyte interface is modified by introducing Li2S additive to harvest stable all-solid-state lithium metal batteries (LMBs).  Cryo-transmission electron microscopy (cryo-TEM) results demonstrate a mosaic interface between poly(ethylene oxide) (PEO) electrolytes and Li metal anodes, in which abundant cryst. grains of Li, Li2O, lithium hydroxide, and Li2CO3 are randomly distributed.  Besides, cryo-TEM visualization, combined with mol. dynamics simulations, reveals that the introduction of Li2S accelerates the decompn. of N(CF3SO2)2- and consequently promotes the formation of abundant LiF nanocrystals in the Li/PEO interface.  The generated LiF is further verified to inhibit the breakage of C-O bonds in the polymer chains and prevents the continuous interface reaction between Li and PEO.  Therefore, the all-solid-state LMBs with the lithium fluoride(LiF)-enriched interface exhibit improved cycling capability and stability in a cell configuration with an ultralong lifespan over 1800 h.  This work is believed to open up a new avenue for rational design of high-performance all-solid-state LMBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq_jNCn8D7cQrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhfRwww5YoKsg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhtlyjt73F&amp;md5=c6f97d1a4f540f49512a32cb08253002</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref16" class="rightTabRefNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit16" data-doi="10.1021/acscentsci.5b00328"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Seh, Z.                         rightTab-cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Highly Reversible Room-Temperature Sodium Metal Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Seh, Zhi Wei; Sun, Jie; Sun, Yongming; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Central Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Owing to its low cost and high natural abundance, sodium metal is among the most promising anode materials for energy storage technologies beyond lithium ion batteries.  However, room-temp. sodium metal anodes suffer from poor reversibility during long-term plating and stripping, mainly due to formation of nonuniform solid electrolyte interphase as well as dendritic growth of sodium metal.  Herein it is reported for the first time that a simple liq. electrolyte, sodium hexafluorophosphate in glymes (mono-, di-, and tetraglyme), can enable highly reversible and nondendritic plating-stripping of sodium metal anodes at room temp.  High av. Coulombic efficiencies of 99.9% were achieved over 300 plating-stripping cycles at 0.5 mA cm-2.  The long-term reversibility was found to arise from the formation of a uniform, inorg. solid electrolyte interphase made of sodium oxide and sodium fluoride, which is highly impermeable to electrolyte solvent and conducive to nondendritic growth.  As a proof of concept, a room-temp. sodium-sulfur battery is demonstrated using this class of electrolytes, paving the way for the development of next-generation, sodium-based energy storage technologies.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrdqXOWzEW74bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liTiJYCvxKalw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhvVGmtrfL&amp;md5=6155e3c781a24b8340081c2ad4444616</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref17" class="rightTabRefNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit17" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2016.09.013"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mishra, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qian, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Engelhard, M. (-30°C) performance of graphite insertion anodes results from a low lithium insertion capacity because polarization or overpotential is higher than the stage transformation plateau potential.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Cycling performance of Li-ion batteries was studied by using electrochem. impedance spectroscopy.  Results showed that total resistance of the Li-ion batteries is mainly composed of bulk resistance (Rb), solid-state interface resistance (Rsei) and charge-transfer resistance (Rct).  During cycling, the Rb and Rsei do not change while the Rct displays two min. in the same voltage regions where the major peaks of differential capacities are present.  The Rct can be linked to kinetics of the battery electrochem. reaction.  In response to the temp. change, the Rb and Rsei vary in a very similar manner, while the Rct shows significant difference.  In the fully charged and discharged states as well as at the low temps. (≤20°), the total resistance of the Li-ion batteries is predominated by the Rct.  Using the term of the Rct, we explained two low-temp. phenomena of the Li-ion battery: (a) charging of a fully discharged battery is much more difficult than discharging of a fully charged battery, and (b) both the power (operating voltage) and energy (delivered capacity) are substantially reduced.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGplYUwg57Nby7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lger__ZDrkEmQ"> &gt;&gt;                         rightTab-cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Materials insights into low-temperature performances of lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhu, Gaolong; Wen, Kechun; Lv, Weiqiang; Zhou, Xingzhi; Liang, Yachun; Yang, Fei; Chen, Zhilin; Zou, Minda; Li, Jinchao; Zhang, Yuqian; He, Weidong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Li-ion batteries (LIBs) have been employed in many fields including cell phones, laptop computers, elec. vehicles (EVs) and stationary energy storage wells due to their high energy d. and pronounced recharge ability.  However, energy and power capabilities of LIBs decrease sharply at low operation temps.  In particular, the charge process becomes extremely sluggish at temps. &lt;-20°, which severely limits the applications of LIBs in some cold areas during winter.  Extensive research showed that the electrolyte/electrode compn. and microstructure are of fundamental importance to low-temp. performances of LIBs.  The authors review the recent findings in the role of electrolytes, anodes, and cathodes in the low temp. performances of LIBs.  The overview aims to understand comprehensively the fundamental origin of low-temp. performances of LIBs from a materials perspective and facilitates the development of high-performance Li-ion battery materials that are operational at a large range of working temps.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGopWzL9s8vIwbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lger__ZDrkEmQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhsFeht7vE&amp;md5=1103aa2d337f63b5eef9bd2899a0973c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref24" class="rightTabRefNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit24" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202001440"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Lithium Difluorophosphate-Based Dual-Salt Low Concentration Electrolytes for Lithium Metal Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The safety hazards and low Coulombic efficiency originating from the growth of lithium dendrites and decompn. of the electrolyte restrict the practical application of Li metal batteries (LMBs).  Inspired by the low cost of low concn. electrolytes (LCEs) in industrial applications, dual-salt LCEs employing 0.1 M Li difluorophosphate (LiDFP) and 0.4 M LiBOB/LiFSI/LiTFSI are proposed to construct a robust and conductive interphase on a Li metal anode.  Compared with the conventional electrolyte using 1 M LiPF6, the ionic cond. of LCEs is reduced but the cond. decrement of the separator immersed in LCEs is moderate, esp. for the LiDFP-LiFSI and LiDFP-LiTFSI electrolytes.  The accurate Coulombic efficiency (CE) of the Li||Cu cells increases from 83.3% (electrolyte using 1 M LiPF6) to 97.6%, 94.5%, and 93.6% for LiDFP-LiBOB, LiDFP-LiFSI, and LiDFP-LiTFSI electrolytes, resp.  The capacity retention of Li||LiFePO4 cells using the LiDFP-LiBOB electrolyte reaches 95.4% along with a CE over 99.8% after 300 cycles at a c.d. of 2.0 mA cm-2 and the capacity reaches 103.7 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of up to 16.0 mA cm-2.  This work provides a dual-salt LCE for practical LMBs and presents a new perspective for the design of electrolytes for LMBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr_0CCahCFtabVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lger__ZDrkEmQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhtF2ltbnI&amp;md5=c7adb92cf06217227ffafaf67b76b9b3</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref25" class="rightTabRefNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit25" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b14878"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, S.-M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Choi, J.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kwon, M.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, K.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium (Na) metal is a promising anode for Na-ion batteries. High-concn. electrolytes (HCE, ≥4 M) consisting of sodium bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide (NaFSI) and ether solvent could ensure the stable cycling of Na metal with high Coulombic efficiency but at the cost of high viscosity, poor wettability, and high salt cost.
